  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4a890242-5b21-4d60-a6cb-e288040c16c5'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initializeSiwa();

<span id="freeTextContainer11638839778769718893">زندگی در پیش‌رو، داستان زندگی پسری چهارده‌ساله است به نام «محمد» که مومو خوانده می‌شود. مادر مومو فاحشه بوده، و فاحشه‌ها در فرانسه حق پرورش فرزند خویش را ندارند، مومو نزد «مادام رُزا» زندگی می‌کند. مادام رزا، پیرزن یهودی لهستانی‌الاصلی‌ست که خود در جوانی فاحشه بوده است. در سال‌های جنگ جهانی دوم در «آشویتس» اردوگاه نازی‌ها اسیر بوده پس از جنگ در فرانسه اقامت می‌‌گُزیند و حا</span>
  <span id="freeText11638839778769718893" style="display:none">زندگی در پیش‌رو، داستان زندگی پسری چهارده‌ساله است به نام «محمد» که مومو خوانده می‌شود. مادر مومو فاحشه بوده، و فاحشه‌ها در فرانسه حق پرورش فرزند خویش را ندارند، مومو نزد «مادام رُزا» زندگی می‌کند. مادام رزا، پیرزن یهودی لهستانی‌الاصلی‌ست که خود در جوانی فاحشه بوده است. در سال‌های جنگ جهانی دوم در «آشویتس» اردوگاه نازی‌ها اسیر بوده پس از جنگ در فرانسه اقامت می‌‌گُزیند و حالا به وقتِ پیری، در ازای دریافتِ ماهانه‌‌ای اندک از چند بچه‌ی مادر ج... نگه‌داری می‌کند. داستان از زبان مومو روایت می‌شود، خواننده از نگاه او با محله‌های فقیر و خارجی‌‌نشینِ پاریس آشنا می‌شود. مومو روایت‌گرِ درد و رنج و خشونتِ حاکم بر انسان‌هاست. او بچه‌ای‌ست که هم‌چون دیگران در این محله، بار غیرقابلِ تحملِ هستی را بر دوش می‌کشد، اما دنیا را به سان آنها نمی‌بیند. زندگی برایش سئوال است، می‌خواهد همه‌چیز را بداند. عشقِ بزرگش دانستن است و همین ذهن‌ کنجکاو است، راوی درون و بیرون.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11638839778769718893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10654622856279176218">La vie devant soi = The Life Before Us, Romain Gary<br/><br/>The Life Before Us, is a novel by French author Romain Gary, who wrote it under the pseudonym of "Emile Ajar". It was originally published in English as Momo then re-published in 1986 as The Life Before Us. It won the Prix Goncourt prize in 1975.<br/><br/>Momo, a Muslim orphan boy who is about 10 years old, lives under the care of an old Jewish woman named Madame Rosa, who was a prisoner at Auschwitz and later became a prostitute in Paris. <br/><br/>Momo's mother a</span>
  <span id="freeText10654622856279176218" style="display:none">La vie devant soi = The Life Before Us, Romain Gary<br /><br />The Life Before Us, is a novel by French author Romain Gary, who wrote it under the pseudonym of "Emile Ajar". It was originally published in English as Momo then re-published in 1986 as The Life Before Us. It won the Prix Goncourt prize in 1975.<br /><br />Momo, a Muslim orphan boy who is about 10 years old, lives under the care of an old Jewish woman named Madame Rosa, who was a prisoner at Auschwitz and later became a prostitute in Paris. <br /><br />Momo's mother abandoned him with Madame Rosa, who is essentially a babysitter for the children of prostitutes. They live on the sixth floor of an apartment building in Belleville, a district of Paris. In their apartment building, Madam Rosa made a small hideout in a cellar, where she keeps artifacts of her Jewish heritage. <br /><br />The young boy tells the story of his life in the orphanage and of his relationship with Madame Rosa as she becomes increasingly sick, culminating with her death, after she had expressed her desire to not die in hospital on life support, saying that she does not want to be a vegetable being forced to live.<br /><br />عنوانها: «زندگی در پیش‌ رو»؛ «زندگی پیش‌ رو»؛ نویسنده: رومن گاری؛ انتشاراتیها: بازتاب نگار، ثالث، جامی؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز دهم نوامبر سال 2010میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: زندگی در پیش‌ رو؛ نویسنده: رومن گاری؛ مترجم: لیلی گلستان؛ تهران، امیرکبیر، 1358؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، بازتاب نگار، 1359؛ در 217ص؛ چاپ دوم 1382؛ چاپ چهارم 1384؛ شابک 9649283161؛ چاپ ششم و هفتم 1386؛ چاپ دهم 1387؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، ثالث، 1393؛ در 235ص، شابک 9789643809508؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان فرانسوی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: زندگی پیش‌ رو؛ نویسنده: رومن گاری؛ مترجم: لیلا عبداللهی؛ تهران، جامی، 1392؛ در 224ص؛ شابک 9786001760914؛ <br /><br />داستان پسربچه ای دهساله است که «مومو» صدایش میکنند، رمان‌های «رومن گاری» گذرگاهی هستند، از زندگی خود ایشان؛ سراسر پیکار است با خویشتن خویش؛ وجود تکه‌ تکه شده اش را می‌توان در شخصیت‌های داستان‌ها باز شناخت<br /><br />مومو در «زندگی در پیش رو» از سه سالگی تا چهارده سالگی خود را واگویی می‌کند، و بیشتر گفته ‌هایش پیرامون «رزا خانم – کسی از او نگهداری می‌کند» است، که از علاقه شدید بین این دو سرچشمه می‌گیرد؛ با توجه به سن و سال و وضعیت «رزا خانم»، می‌توان از این زاویه، کتاب را مرثیه ‌ای برای سالخوردگی، و عمر رفته نیز، پذیرا شد، اما برای راوی، عمده «زندگی در پیش رو» است…؛<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 06/07/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_1745147195" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1745147195" />
    <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro"><img alt="Michael Finocchiaro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576692048p2/48483884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1745147195?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Finocchiaro" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro">Michael Finocchiaro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/635150.La_vie_devant_soi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=goncourt">goncourt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=read-in-french">read-in-french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=french-20th-c">french-20th-c</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1745147195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11005811034747028371">One of the most beautiful and optimistic books I have ever read, The Life Before Us is a masterpiece. Romain Gary wrote it under a pseudonym, Emile Ajar, and thus became to only person to win the French Goncourt Prize twice. In fact, it was only after his tragic death that the identity of the mysterious but astounding write Ajar was revealed to by Romain Gary. <br/><br/>The story is told my Momo, an infant of prostitution in an illegal boarding house run by the aging Madame Rosa, well past her prime but w</span>
  <span id="freeText11005811034747028371" style="display:none">One of the most beautiful and optimistic books I have ever read, The Life Before Us is a masterpiece. Romain Gary wrote it under a pseudonym, Emile Ajar, and thus became to only person to win the French Goncourt Prize twice. In fact, it was only after his tragic death that the identity of the mysterious but astounding write Ajar was revealed to by Romain Gary. <br /><br />The story is told my Momo, an infant of prostitution in an illegal boarding house run by the aging Madame Rosa, well past her prime but who still "defended herself" from time to time. Momo lives with other children of prostitutes and is a fount of wisdom and consciousness that - despite his hilarious vocabulary of words used in the wrong sense but which reveal even more about his sensibility - surprises and amuses us at every turn of the page:<br /><br />"The first thing I can tell you is that we lived on the 6th floor without an elevator and for Madame Rosa with all those pounds she carried on only two legs, it was a real source of daily life, with all its worried and pains." (p.1)<br /><br />"i believe that the injust sleep the best because they don't give a shit, while the righteous cannot close their eyes and feel guilty about everything." (p. 39)<br /><br />"'It is not necessary to have reasons to be scared, Momo'<br />That is something I never forgot because it is the truest thing i ever heard." (p.63)<br /><br />"I think that to live, you need to start young, because later you lose all your value and nobody will give you a break." (p.88)<br /><br />"I was so happy that I wanted to die because you must seize happiness when it is there before you" (p. 96)<br /><br />I could not possibly render justice to the story of Momo and Madame Rosa with Momo's friends Monsieur Hamil and his book of Victor Hugo (which in his dementia he confuses with the Qu'ran), Madame Lolo (the Senegalese ex-boxer transvestite who works the Bois de Boulogne) or the wonderful Nadine...I encourage you - particularly in this dark moment for world politics at the eve of the Drumpftastrophe- to take refuge with Momo and Madame Rosa. Like Momo says in closing the book, "We must love."<br /><br /><br />(All translations are mine from the Folio Classics edition in the original French)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11005811034747028371" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2468541580" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2468541580" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2468541580?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9783125972698" href="/book/show/5390215-la-vie-devant-soi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=prix-goncourt">prix-goncourt</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2468541580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15745510691408827459">Absolutely stunning narrator voice!<br/><br/>Romain Gary has so many different nuances (and pseudonyms!) that each book is a complete surprise. This one is no exception, and it manages the impossible balance of being incredibly brutal and heartbreakingly sweet and amazingly funny.<br/><br/>What is the brutal part?<br/><br/>A young boy grows up with an old lady in Belleville, a poor and crime-ridden part of Paris. He learns far too early to navigate a world of whores and pimps and drug addicts.<br/><br/>What is the sweet part?<br/><br/>Despite t</span>
  <span id="freeText15745510691408827459" style="display:none">Absolutely stunning narrator voice!<br /><br />Romain Gary has so many different nuances (and pseudonyms!) that each book is a complete surprise. This one is no exception, and it manages the impossible balance of being incredibly brutal and heartbreakingly sweet and amazingly funny.<br /><br />What is the brutal part?<br /><br />A young boy grows up with an old lady in Belleville, a poor and crime-ridden part of Paris. He learns far too early to navigate a world of whores and pimps and drug addicts.<br /><br />What is the sweet part?<br /><br />Despite the hopelessness of the situation, there is friendship and love and tolerance in the story, where an ex-boxer and champion from Sénégal can become a caring woman ("except for some natural limitations") who works as a transvestite prostitute but knocks out the dangerous maniacs ... where a Jewish obese ex-prostitute who survived Auschwitz can raise children according to the religion their prostitute/pimp parents wish for them ... where illness and weakness is dealt with because life doesn't forgive ... <br /><br />What is the funny part?<br /><br />The voice of the boy is unbelievable. He is so clever and yet so uneducated. He knows things most grown-ups have never heard of, and yet he misunderstands the words and mixes up the concepts. He analyses the world according to Belleville code, and the reader follows in bafflement. <br /><br />In the end, there is brutally funny sweetness!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15745510691408827459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3615603011" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3615603011" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3615603011?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811209618" href="/book/show/58177.The_Life_Before_Us">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=the-french">the-french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3615603011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15458335227294820093"><b>”I had fun for a while scaring cars by crossing in front of them at the last minute. People don’t like to run children over, and it gave me a kick to know I was making them nervous. They’d step on the brake like a ton of bricks to keep from hurting you, which isn’t much, but it’s better than nothing. I’d have liked to scare them even worse, but I wasn’t up to it yet. I hadn’t quite decided whether to join up with the police or the terrorists, all in good time. I’d see.”</b><br/><br/>This passage reminded me o</span>
  <span id="freeText15458335227294820093" style="display:none"><b>”I had fun for a while scaring cars by crossing in front of them at the last minute. People don’t like to run children over, and it gave me a kick to know I was making them nervous. They’d step on the brake like a ton of bricks to keep from hurting you, which isn’t much, but it’s better than nothing. I’d have liked to scare them even worse, but I wasn’t up to it yet. I hadn’t quite decided whether to join up with the police or the terrorists, all in good time. I’d see.”</b><br /><br />This passage reminded me of when I was a kid and my younger brother and I would stand out on either side of #9 Highway at dusk and act like we were holding a rope across the road. We always thought it was a victory when we got someone to stop. We would laugh until we cried whenever someone would fling an expletive at us as they roared onward. <br /><br />Momo is for all intents and purposes an orphan. He is one of a number of kids being taken care of by a fat Jewish woman named Madame Rosa, a woman who peddled her ass until she became too fat and ugly to be of interest to her clients anymore. To make ends meet, she starts taking in the offspring of whores to raise. They will send her money when they can, and she will keep their kids out of the hands of the government. <i>”Minors are wonderfully protected in France, and when there’s nobody to look after them the government throws them in jail for safekeeping.”</i> One of the biggest fears of Momo and Madame Rosa is a visit from social services. They will certainly sweep in and kidnap these kids to be hauled off to abusive institutions.<br /><br />Romain Gary came back on my radar when I watched the movie <i>Seberg</i> (2019), starring Kristen Stewart. Gary was married to Jean Seberg from 1962-1970, though they remained confidants for the rest of their lives. When the actor playing Gary showed up on screen, it was one of those moments that still happens to me too often...why haven’t I ever read this guy? Finally, I’ve rectified that, and there is now a checkmark next to Gary’s name on my lifetime reading list. <br /><br />This book certainly has an identity crisis. It was titled <i>The Life Before Us</i> and later, when the movie came out based on the book, called <i>Madame Rosa</i> (1978). The book also briefly changed its name as well. To make things even more confusing, this book was originally published under the pen name Emile Ajar. Gary was so successful at hiding his identity with this name that he won the Prix Goncourt twice, once under his real name and as Ajar. A writer can only win this award once, so he had his cousin, who was publicly playing Emile Ajar, refuse the award. He and his cousin eventually had a major falling out over money, and Gary was decidedly cruel during the ongoing argument. Nothing like an angry writer letting loose his quiver of barbed words. Though, telling his cousin that he fucked his mother, even if it was true, was certainly a talented writer resorting to vulgarity to win a point. There is this point in the story when Momo says: <i>”I have always been someone else.”</i> That might be Romain Gary telling us why he felt so compelled to have another identity. <br /><br />Seberg was hounded by the FBI for the last years of her life because of her association and sympathy for the Black Panthers. She died from an apparant suicide in her car in 1979. A little over a year later, Romain Gary also committed suicide and insisted in his note that his decision had nothing to do with Seberg’s death. I can still remember the first time I saw Seberg in the Godard movie <i>Breathless</i> (1960). She will forever be running down a Paris street in my mind. <br /><br />Madame Rosa was in Auschwitz, and to remind herself that life can always be worse, she keeps a picture of Hitler under her bed. Whenever she becomes too depressed, Momo will grab that picture and thrust it under her nose to bring a smile to her face. She is deathly afraid of landing in the hands of doctors. <i>”Momo, I don’t want to live just for the sake of medical science. I know I’m losing my mind and I don’t want to hang on for years in a coma just to make the doctors happy. For thirty-five years I gave my ass to the customers, and I’m not giving it to the doctors now.”</i> Momo loves spending time with her because, even though she is Jewish and he is an Arab, he knows they are the <i>”same class of shit.”</i> He does everything he can to keep her alive. <br /><br />One of my favorite characters is a transvestite named Madame Lola, who lives in the same building as Momo, who once was a boxer but now peddles her ass. Whenever a guy tries to get too rough with her, he doesn't realize he is taking on way more than he can handle. Lola helps Rosa and Moma financially and emotionally as they try to navigate the last months of Rosa’s life. <br /><br />This is a short book and not really the type of subject matter that would catch my attention, but I have to say there are numerous poignant, sharply defined scenes that will certainly linger with me for a long time. It makes me believe that Gary was able to tap into a different part of himself when he wrote under the guise of Emile Ajar. As James Laughlin says in the Afterword: <b>“Gary was Ajar...but who <i>was</i> Ajar?”</b> For astute people who really knew Gary, which I wonder if anyone really knew him, there are clues in the texts of his Ajar books to reveal his identity. He admitted he was Emile Ajar in his suicide note. In the end, he embraced the other half of himself. I can only hope that at that moment he felt whole. <br /><br />If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br />I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a> and an Instagram account <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.instagram.com/jeffreykeeten/">https://www.instagram.com/jeffreykeeten/</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15458335227294820093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3445370880" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3445370880" />
    <a title="Kalliope" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope"><img alt="Kalliope" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428482885p2/3593962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3445370880?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalliope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalliope" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope">Kalliope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/635150.La_vie_devant_soi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=fiction-french">fiction-french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=20-century">20-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3445370880" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14266320041600703728"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596906288i/29935548._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I still associate Summer with reading French books. That’s a routine we had when growing up. The shadow of Romain Gary belongs to those Summers-- with all the enchantment that memories from one’s youth hold for all of us-- since his was one of names on my father’s bookshelves. So, I am certain I have read some of his works but cannot remember which. No matter, I am delighted I have picked his books up again, for this novel has been one of the most memorable reads this year. <br/><br/><i>La vie devant soi</i> was</span>
  <span id="freeText14266320041600703728" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596906288i/29935548._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I still associate Summer with reading French books. That’s a routine we had when growing up. The shadow of Romain Gary belongs to those Summers-- with all the enchantment that memories from one’s youth hold for all of us-- since his was one of names on my father’s bookshelves. So, I am certain I have read some of his works but cannot remember which. No matter, I am delighted I have picked his books up again, for this novel has been one of the most memorable reads this year. <br><br><i>La vie devant soi</i> was published in 1975 under the penname Émile Ajar and it won the Goncourt that same year -- it was chosen over one by Patrick Modiano. Gary had already received the Prize in 1956 with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/118967.Les_Racines_du_ciel" title="Les Racines du ciel by Romain Gary" rel="nofollow noopener">Les Racines du ciel</a> and since this prize can be awarded only once to a given author plus the fact that Gary was going through a difficult time with the literary critics, prompted him to present the book under pseudonym. Who was behind Ajar was not found out until after Gary’s suicide in 1980.<br><br>And the fact that this could happen, that no one suspected who was the real author behind a novel that had such immediate and great success, is the best proof of what for me is one of the most attractive features of this novel: the voice of the narrator. An excellent writer is the one who can create a voice that completely defines the personality, the mind, of a fictional character, and completely disappear behind it. <br><br>So, when we hear Momo, a Muslim (Mohamed) of about ten to fourteen years old (for his age remains a mystery for a portion of the book) describe his world, his life, at the pension run by Madame Rose, an elderly former prostitute who, as a Polish Jew, had survived through Auschwitz before she came to live in Paris, we are completely captivated by him. For although his universe is that of a working-class district in North-eastern Paris, the candour and alertness with which he observes those around him, and his reasoning of how and why things happen in a certain way, one forgets that behind this invented Momo there is an adult writer of an entirely different background. <br><br>Momo becomes particularly endearing when he uses expressions, often euphemistic, that he has heard around him and has completely assimilated them without fully understanding what is behind them. The most notorious is the one he has learnt from Madame Rose when she refers to the other women <i>qui se défendaient (avec son cul)</i>, and that he then often uses when he figures out how other adults also ‘defend themselves’. Endearing also in Momo is his heart. His gut love for the decaying Madame Rose who becomes more and more preposterous, not just to us, but to him too, is almost chimerical. And that love is the driver in the story. <br><br>The portrayal of this impoverished society is enriched by the gallery of personalities, and if one needs a scenario, the best is offered by Madame Rose’s apartment at the top of building of six storeys with no lift. Climbing to her home is for most of the characters, but in particular for Madame Rose herself, like ascending the Calvary. To me the most striking personality in this gallery is the angel-like Madame Lola, a travesty who ‘defends herself’ as a prostitute, who was a former boxer in Senegal, and who is quite successful in her career being able to provide intermittent but needed assistance to Madame Rose and her cares. <br><br>After such a memorable read, I am looking forward to discovering more of Gary’s voices and, may be, I will unearth which one I had already read in one of those past Summers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14266320041600703728" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2812149319680560046" style="display:none">This is the story of 'Madame Rosa'...a classic French book, written by Romain Gary, an American screenwriter who gained fame as a French writer. He's won many <br />literary and honors awards in France.<br /><br />There is a a stage play of this story ...I haven't seen it.. but it would be easy to visualize.<br /><br />A wonderful evocation of a child's mind and experience.<br />Funny, disturbing...sad...honest...unconventional environment. <br />This novel touches our heart while captivating our mind. ( a simple tale of many layers). <br />No-nonsense wasted words! <br />Most of the novel takes place in the apartment of Madame Rosa. She is Jewish, <br />an ex-prostitute. obese, and dying. <br />The relationship between Momo and Madame Rosa against the most seedy <br />conditions is profoundly moving. One does not stop thinking about this book just <br />because you've finished reading it. <br />The minor characters also memorable.....Dr. Katz, ( Madame Rosa's Doctor), Banania, ( young black boy), <br />Monsieur Hamil, ( sold carpets), and Arthur, ( an umbrella--Momo's best friend). <br /><br />Quotes:<br />"Life can be beautiful except nobody really knows how to make it that way and meanwhile we've got to live".<br /><br />"You don't need a reason to be afraid"<br /><br />"can somebody live without love?"<br /><br />"nightmares are what dreams turn into with age". <br /><br />*part of this book was based on the authors life.<br />It's very sad that he died so young...leaving a suicide note 'for the press'...in December, 1980. <br />Romain Gary was an extraordinary writer. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_206529400" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/206529400" />
    <a title="Lee Klein " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein"><img alt="Lee Klein " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594811741p2/90786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/206529400?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Klein " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Klein " href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein">Lee Klein </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811209618" href="/book/show/58177.The_Life_Before_Us">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer206529400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3365312622407210677">Portrait of the artist as the young Algerian son of a prostitute raised in 1970 paris slums by an obese 67-year-old Holocaust survivor/retired whore and educated by Senegalese trannies and wise old men whose long experience must be respected. First-person narration by a well-meaning, sensitive, street-smart 10-year-old/14-year-old named Momo, whose best friend is a dressed-up umbrella named Arthur and who has too much pride to peddle his ass. Lots of LOL malapropisms (esp. re: "dramatized" and "</span>
  <span id="freeText3365312622407210677" style="display:none">Portrait of the artist as the young Algerian son of a prostitute raised in 1970 paris slums by an obese 67-year-old Holocaust survivor/retired whore and educated by Senegalese trannies and wise old men whose long experience must be respected. First-person narration by a well-meaning, sensitive, street-smart 10-year-old/14-year-old named Momo, whose best friend is a dressed-up umbrella named Arthur and who has too much pride to peddle his ass. Lots of LOL malapropisms (esp. re: "dramatized" and "abortion"), old-fashioned black humor, and general corrupted innocent joyousness that comes off as legit poignancy. No way these people didn't exist: willing suspension of disbelief in full merefreakin' Parisian effect. Readerly affection evoked by author's soulfulness, humor, transparency, and running jokes, pet phrases, silly motifs, tics. Highly recommended to fans of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/394208.Christian_TeBordo" title="Christian TeBordo" rel="nofollow noopener">Christian TeBordo</a> -- often reminded me of a distant relation. Read this because it was a highly rated novel translated by Ralph Manheim. The title should just be "Madame Rosa," not the unrememberable "The Life Before Us." Also, I generally cringe when it comes to lit involving kids, especially when it's narrated by them, but now I can add this to the short list of novels narrated by or involving children that don't make me nauseous (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/230514.The_Notebook__The_Proof__The_Third_Lie_Three_Novels" title="The Notebook, The Proof, The Third Lie Three Novels by Ágota Kristóf" rel="nofollow noopener">The Notebook, The Proof, The Third Lie: Three Novels</a>; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/190372.The_Last_Samurai" title="The Last Samurai by Helen DeWitt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Samurai</a>; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/289113.His_Dark_Materials_Trade_Paper_Boxed_Set_The_Golden_Compass__The_Subtle_Knife__The_Amber_Spyglass__His_Dark_Materials___1_3_" title="His Dark Materials Trade Paper Boxed Set The Golden Compass, The Subtle Knife, The Amber Spyglass (His Dark Materials, #1-3) by Philip Pullman" rel="nofollow noopener">His Dark Materials Trade Paper Boxed Set: The Golden Compass, The Subtle Knife, The Amber Spyglass</a>; and maybe like Huck Finn and Tom Sawyer?). Of note, the author was married to Jean Seberg, famous short-haired actress in Godard's "Breathless."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3365312622407210677" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText4014190163589016631" style="display:none">Some are born to sweet delight… It isn’t about the hero of this novel… And although adolescent Momo lived among those whose profession was to bring delights to the others his home was the sort of a gloomy demimonde orphanage… <br />“Madame Rosa had gray hair that was falling too, because it had stopped caring very much one way or the other. She had more ass and bosom than anybody you ever heard of, and when she looked at herself in the glass, she always made a big smile, like she was trying to vamp herself.”<br /><b>Romain Gary</b> was an outstanding writer and he is the only author to win the Prix Goncourt twice – the second time when he has written his novel <i>The Life Before Us</i> under the nom de plume of <b>Émile Ajar</b>. The book is a brilliant coming-of-age drama and there is also an excellent movie adaptation of this novel titled <i>Madame Rosa</i>.</span>

<div id="review_606633661" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/606633661" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/606633661?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9783125972698" href="/book/show/5390215-la-vie-devant-soi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=paris">paris</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=dementia">dementia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer606633661" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16298601474587171456"><b>Heartrendering survival story in multicultural Paris</b><br/>The special thing about "La vie devant soi" is the charming storyteller: the 10-year old Momo, living in Paris, son of a prostitute (which he never knew). He's now living with Mme Rosa, on the sixth floor in a very multicultural district of Paris, in the 1970's. Mme Rosa too is a former prostitute, of jewish descent (she survived Auschwitz). <br/><br/>In his own words Momo describes the very difficult situation of the ageing Rosa, his love and care for h</span>
  <span id="freeText16298601474587171456" style="display:none"><b>Heartrendering survival story in multicultural Paris</b><br />The special thing about "La vie devant soi" is the charming storyteller: the 10-year old Momo, living in Paris, son of a prostitute (which he never knew). He's now living with Mme Rosa, on the sixth floor in a very multicultural district of Paris, in the 1970's. Mme Rosa too is a former prostitute, of jewish descent (she survived Auschwitz). <br /><br />In his own words Momo describes the very difficult situation of the ageing Rosa, his love and care for her, his conversations with arabs and africans in the neighbourhood. The way he does that is endearing: in a faulty kind of French, registering the challenges and unjust situations in a marginal part of the French capital, and reacting to them in a very wise and disarming way. But then Rosa gets sick, Momo is distressed by the rapidly deteriorating evolution of her condition, but he takes care of her with uttermost tenderness. The outcome is dramatic and hilarious at the same time. <br /><br />This is a multi-layered, heartrendering story about aging in a modern city and the innocence of youth. The curious thing is that Gary published this book under the name of Émile Ajar, formally to avoid stereotypical reviews, but at the same time this move is completely in line with the deceitful personality of Gary himself. It won him a second prestigious Goncourt, which normally is accorded just once to an author. Perhaps it's not his best, but it certainly is an endearing read. It shows that even in the midst of the most difficult of times, plain humanity can prevail.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16298601474587171456" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2603103008" />
    <a title="Jonathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/531153-jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509135468p2/531153.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<div id="review_1085423248" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1085423248" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1895570-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484874117p2/1895570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1085423248?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811209618" href="/book/show/58177.The_Life_Before_Us">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=france">france</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1085423248" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6654427286726375061">Robert Browning's Rabbi Ben Ezra was wrong: "Come grow old with me/The best is yet to be." Not hardly. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/32807.Romain_Gary' title='Romain Gary' rel='nofollow noopener'>Romain Gary</a>, writing as Emile Ajar, in <b>The Life Before Us</b> [a.k.a. <b>Madame Rosa</b>], writes the definitive book about old age.<br/><br/>In Paris's Belleville, Madame Rosa lives raising the children of Jewish, North African, and Black African prostitutes, who send her a monthly pittance to care for them. Prominent among them is the 14-year-old Mohammed, who prefers to be called Momo, is fiercely loyal to the o</span>
  <span id="freeText6654427286726375061" style="display:none">Robert Browning's Rabbi Ben Ezra was wrong: "Come grow old with me/The best is yet to be." Not hardly. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/32807.Romain_Gary" title="Romain Gary" rel="nofollow noopener">Romain Gary</a>, writing as Emile Ajar, in <b>The Life Before Us</b> [a.k.a. <b>Madame Rosa</b>], writes the definitive book about old age.<br /><br />In Paris's Belleville, Madame Rosa lives raising the children of Jewish, North African, and Black African prostitutes, who send her a monthly pittance to care for them. Prominent among them is the 14-year-old Mohammed, who prefers to be called Momo, is fiercely loyal to the old Jewish woman. Unfortunately, Madame Rosa is at the end of her tether. She managed to escape being killed in Auschwitz, but she is falling to pieces both physically and mentally. It is touching to see how she is cared for Momo, by a Senegalese transvestite, an elderly Jewish doctor, and other people in the neighborhood.<br /><br />Madame Rosa's greatest fear is to go to the hospital, where she would be made to live as a vegetable. In her own words:<blockquote>They're going to keep me alive by force, Momo. That's what they always do in the hospital. They've got laws that tell them to. I don't want to live any longer than necessary, and it's not necessary already. Even for Jews there's a limit. They'll torture me to keep me from dying. They've got a shebang called the Medical Association just for that.</blockquote>Meanwhile, Momo is doing everything possible to avoid being picked up by the French social welfare authorities and raised in an institution. <br /><br />This is perhaps one of the most humane novels ever written, and it is exquisitely translated by Ralph Manheim. It is easily one of the best books of any kind I have real all year, and I have read over a hundred so far. So sad, too, because its author was a Lithuanian bastard who always felt he didn't belong, and who committed suicide in 1980. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6654427286726375061" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_733024844" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/733024844" />
    <a title="Janet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1590757-janet"><img alt="Janet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1223150832p2/1590757.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/733024844?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janet" href="/user/show/1590757-janet">Janet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811209618" href="/book/show/58177.The_Life_Before_Us">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1590757-janet?shelf=memorable-characters">memorable-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1590757-janet?shelf=death">death</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1590757-janet?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer733024844" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17765084402828910610"><br/>Synopsis: <u>The Life Before Us</u> is the story of Momo, an orphaned Arab boy and his devotion to Madame Rosa, a dying 68 year old, 220 lb. survivor of Auschwitz. Rosa, a former prostitute, has created a second career for herself providing room and board to the children of other whores in the ethnic ghettos of Paris. As her physical and mental health decline, Momo determines to support her in any way he can. The supporting cast is rounded out with transvestites, pimps and witch doctors. This is a book</span>
  <span id="freeText17765084402828910610" style="display:none"><br />Synopsis: <u>The Life Before Us</u> is the story of Momo, an orphaned Arab boy and his devotion to Madame Rosa, a dying 68 year old, 220 lb. survivor of Auschwitz. Rosa, a former prostitute, has created a second career for herself providing room and board to the children of other whores in the ethnic ghettos of Paris. As her physical and mental health decline, Momo determines to support her in any way he can. The supporting cast is rounded out with transvestites, pimps and witch doctors. This is a book guaranteed to leave you completely unsettled. <br /><br />Described by critics as a “<i>sensitive, slightly macabre love story”</i> I was reminded of the 1981 Brazilian film <u>Pixote</u>, another unflinching look at growing up on the streets through the eyes of a world-weary young boy. These are stories of what stands in for love when love is absent. These are stories of tremendous brutality with incidences of humbling beauty and grace.<br /><br />As with all books the reader’s frame of mind influences what one will take away. For me, it was Gary’s treatise on the public health care and welfare systems as they existed in France in 1975. The fear of the loss of independence is the story’s primary driver – Momo that he will be put in foster care and Rosa that she will be put in a hospital to live indefinitely as a human vegetable. My husband and I have been watching the steady decline of his mother, now 88, who outlived her money by age 75 and is now going through ours at an alarming rate as her needs for care escalate. There really is nothing quite like walking down the ever shortening pier of your own approaching dotage while fanning life’s embers for one who no longer recognizes you. Thus, the rhetorical questions: What good is longevity if there is no quality? Does a half or quarter life constitute living? And, who pays for it all? Below are a couple quotes from <u>The Life Before Us</u> that particularly resonated with me:<br /><br />“<i>Medicine has to have the last word and fight to the last ditch to prevent God’s will from being done.”</i><br /><br />“<i>In my opinion there’s nothing crummier than ramming life down the throats of defenseless people who’ve had enough.”</i><br /><br />Gary’s style is immediate and familiar. Some may rail against his gallows type humor but it lends itself to an easy accessibility I like as evidenced by the book’s opening paragraph:<br /><br />“<i>The first thing I have to tell you is that we lived on the seventh-floor walk-up, so you can take my word for it that Madame Rosa, with all the pounds she had to lug around with her, had more than her share of daily life with all its sorrows and cares. She said so too, whenever she wasn’t complaining about something else, because to make matters worse she was Jewish. Her health wasn’t so good either, and I can tell you right now that if ever a woman deserved an elevator it was Madame Rosa.”</i><br /><br /> Gary who won France’s premier literary prize, the Prix Goncourt, in 1956 under his own name, wrote and published <u>The Life Before Us</u> in 1975 under the pen name, Emile Ajar. In doing so, he became the only author to have won the Prix Goncourt twice though illegally as the prize is only awarded once to an author. The back story here is that Gary paid his nephew to impersonate Emile Ajar - they became estranged when Gary refused to split the book’s royalties with him. Gary is perhaps best known as the second husband of actress, Jean Seberg who made headlines when she was tied to the Black Panthers and it was rumored her child was fathered by a member of that organization rather than by Gary. She committed suicide in 1979 and Gary committed suicide the following year. The last line of his suicide note reading, “<i>There’s no better way to say it, I have expressed myself completely.”</i>. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17765084402828910610" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2376161358?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alessandro Speciale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alessandro Speciale" href="/user/show/11318428-alessandro-speciale">Alessandro Speciale</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788854500341" href="/book/show/9652255-la-vita-davanti-a-s">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2376161358" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1963205553354624998">Well, this was an unexpected miracle. Drawing a list of the "themes" or "issues" this book touches upon makes for the bucket list of the definitive socially conscious, morally righteous book: Arabs and Jews, euthanasia, dementia, prostitution, racism, immigration, gay rights -- and that's not even comprehensive. In short, a summary of this book would guarantee that no one touches it from a mile away. And yet, and yet. It is wonderfully amoral, light hearted and profoundly and simply human story.</span>
  <span id="freeText1963205553354624998" style="display:none">Well, this was an unexpected miracle. Drawing a list of the "themes" or "issues" this book touches upon makes for the bucket list of the definitive socially conscious, morally righteous book: Arabs and Jews, euthanasia, dementia, prostitution, racism, immigration, gay rights -- and that's not even comprehensive. In short, a summary of this book would guarantee that no one touches it from a mile away. And yet, and yet. It is wonderfully amoral, light hearted and profoundly and simply human story. It doesn't try to provoke, to stir, to denounce. Maybe because it was written by a wonderfully amoral man who lived in one life what would be enough to fill ten lives. Or maybe because it was written in the 70s in France, and the author was a friend of Camus. In short, a wonderful, powerful, deeply cynical and deeply heartful book. Highly recommended</span>
  <a data-text-id="1963205553354624998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer217837195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16020612569976089076">Incredible prose: the perfect length and feeling for a rainy fall afternoon. <br/><br/>It's a pleasure to read a simple story like this one. The simplicity grabs you, right from the first page. The main character, little Momo is the most charming protagonist in my recent readings (I'd like to get him and the little boy from <i>Extremely Loud and Incredibly Close</i> together in one room - what a pair of moppets!). Momo, an orphaned whore's son, is the right mix of wide-eyed newborn babe and street urchin who's s</span>
  <span id="freeText16020612569976089076" style="display:none">Incredible prose: the perfect length and feeling for a rainy fall afternoon. <br /><br />It's a pleasure to read a simple story like this one. The simplicity grabs you, right from the first page. The main character, little Momo is the most charming protagonist in my recent readings (I'd like to get him and the little boy from <i>Extremely Loud and Incredibly Close</i> together in one room - what a pair of moppets!). Momo, an orphaned whore's son, is the right mix of wide-eyed newborn babe and street urchin who's seen it all. The story revolves around Momo's role as a caretake for his own foster mother, Madame Rosa. Their relationship is touching without being overly sentimental. Madame Rosa declines and Momo gains four years in a minute, literally, figuratively, he becomes a little man. You want to take him in- literally pull him from the pages and feed him a bowl of vanilla ice cream- but you know he'd never have you and that just makes you long for him more. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16020612569976089076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1642668784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1642668784" />
    <a title="Martin Yankov" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3293999-martin-yankov"><img alt="Martin Yankov" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1382435807p2/3293999.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1642668784?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martin Yankov" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martin Yankov" href="/user/show/3293999-martin-yankov">Martin Yankov</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/635150.La_vie_devant_soi">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1642668784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11970200815368187066">This is probably the saddest book I've ever read. <br/>The thing is, it's not written as something horribly sad. The style is actually quite comedic. The novel tells the story of a young boy growing up in an immigrant neighborhood in Paris. He lives with an old dying Jewish woman - an ex-prostitute and a Holocaust survivor. He lives in a terrifying world, but since he is a child, he knows no other world, so he speaks about that harsh life in a completely casual manner, as if there's nothing strange o</span>
  <span id="freeText11970200815368187066" style="display:none">This is probably the saddest book I've ever read. <br />The thing is, it's not written as something horribly sad. The style is actually quite comedic. The novel tells the story of a young boy growing up in an immigrant neighborhood in Paris. He lives with an old dying Jewish woman - an ex-prostitute and a Holocaust survivor. He lives in a terrifying world, but since he is a child, he knows no other world, so he speaks about that harsh life in a completely casual manner, as if there's nothing strange or unusual to it. And that's more than sad - that's completely horrifying. <br /><br />The books deals with multiple serious themes like immigration, prostitution, poverty and euthanasia. It is set in Paris, but that's not the Paris we know from the sweet romantic movies. It's the dark side of Paris - a place so depressing that you don't even want to think about it. And the book tells a story about love. Even in that ugly, unbelievably horrible world, there is love. It's the love of a lonely Muslim child for the fat decaying Jewish woman he lives with. And it is the beautiful with the saddest kind of beauty. <br /><br />It is a great book. A must-read, I'd say. But it won't just make you cry - it'll make you feel depressed for days. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11970200815368187066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1117265666" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1117265666" />
    <a title="Maria Carmo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3054983-maria-carmo"><img alt="Maria Carmo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1261144324p2/3054983.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1117265666?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria Carmo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria Carmo" href="/user/show/3054983-maria-carmo">Maria Carmo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789896760359" href="/book/show/10770048-uma-vida-sua-frente">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3054983-maria-carmo?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3054983-maria-carmo?shelf=childhood-and-youth-books">childhood-and-youth-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3054983-maria-carmo?shelf=novel-romance-revealing-historica">novel-romance-revealing-historica</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3054983-maria-carmo?shelf=literature-and-art">literature-and-art</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3054983-maria-carmo?shelf=life-changing-self-discovery-self">life-changing-self-discovery-self</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1117265666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12280584779443896785">If there is "different" book, it is this one. The "voice" in which the story is told is radically diverse of what is current - the young boy telling the story seems to have no sort of "filter" - he is the observer of his own life with Madame Rosa, coloured by the fact that he leaves in the Paris "bas fond", among prostitutes, travesties and people who are illegal migrants... The love between Momo and Madame Rosa does not prevent his critical look upon life's events and ways... It is, apparently,</span>
  <span id="freeText12280584779443896785" style="display:none">If there is "different" book, it is this one. The "voice" in which the story is told is radically diverse of what is current - the young boy telling the story seems to have no sort of "filter" - he is the observer of his own life with Madame Rosa, coloured by the fact that he leaves in the Paris "bas fond", among prostitutes, travesties and people who are illegal migrants... The love between Momo and Madame Rosa does not prevent his critical look upon life's events and ways... It is, apparently, a blunt look - but there is so much innocence in his look that one cannot misinterpret his bluntness... And the tenderness is revealed, therefore, without excess emotionalism or sentimentality. Love and tenderness and despair colour the separation of Momo and Madame Rosa. Absolutely a good read.<br /><br />Maria Carmo,<br /><br />Lisbon 30 November 2014. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12280584779443896785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('42NeiVOeGf7GVmvYZJycjA6/d3e1jCym8f/IOEJ82dbs+w/Q9pt0eIt9MxX89yze0OZlLhKopRFZF3Vl+E4mqg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iSIr4jAcG1cwhENXKg5VvdaK4TsFCvINrC1rDFnv8C2Gunq7lRl20X2vG5qyZeXvCNPzYqIue7oExdZR490PUQ==')})
  }

Le couple s\'installe à Meknès, ville de garnison et de colons, où le système de ségrégation coloniale s\'applique avec rigueur. Amine récupère ses terres, rocailleuses ingrates et commence alors une période très dure pour la famille. Mathilde accouche de deux enfants : Aïcha et Sélim. Au prix de nombreux sacrifices et vexations, Amine parvient à organiser son domaine, en s\'alliant avec un médecin hongrois, Dragan Palosi, qui va devenir un ami très proche. Mathilde se sent étouffée par le climat rigoriste du Maroc, par sa solitude à la ferme, par la méfiance qu\'elle inspire en tant qu\'étrangère et par le manque d\'argent. Les relations entre les colons et les indigènes sont très tendues, et Amine se trouve pris entre deux feux : marié à une Française, propriétaire terrien employant des ouvriers marocains, il est assimilé aux colons par les autochtones, et méprisé et humilié par les Français parce qu\'il est marocain. Il est fier de sa femme, de son courage, de sa beauté particulière, de son fort tempérament, mais il en a honte aussi car elle ne fait pas preuve de la modestie ni de la soumission convenables. Aïcha grandit dans ce climat de violence, suivant l\'éducation que lui prodiguent les Soeurs à Meknès, où elle fréquente des fillettes françaises issues de familles riches qui l\'humilient. Selma, la soeur d\'Amine, nourrit des rêves de liberté sans cesse brimés par les hommes qui l\'entourent. Alors qu\'Amine commence à récolter les fruits de son travail harassant, des émeutes éclatent, les plantations sont incendiées : le roman se clôt sur des scènes de violence inaugurant l\'accès du pays On se marie à l\'église comme on <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16211292134909649549\" style=\"display:none\">Chick, Alise, Chloé et Colin passent leur temps à dire des choses rigolotes, à écouter Duke Ellington et à patiner. Dans ce monde où les pianos sont des mélangeurs à cocktails, la réalité semble ne pas avoir de prise. On se marie à l\'église comme on va à la fête foraine et on ignore le travail, qui se réduit à une usine monstrueuse faisant tache sur le paysage. Pied de nez aux conventions romanesques et à la morale commune, L\'Écume des jours est un délice verbal et un festin poétique. Jeux de mots, néologismes, décalages incongrus... Vian surenchérit sans cesse, faisant naître comme un vertige chez le lecteur hébété, qui sourit quand il peut. Mais le véritable malaise vient d\'ailleurs : ces adolescents éternels à la sensibilité exacerbée constituent des victimes de choix. L\'obsession consumériste de Chick, née d\'une idolâtrie frénétique pour un certain Jean-Sol Partre, semble vouloir dire que le bonheur ne saurait durer. En effet, l\'asphyxie gagne du terrain, et l\'on assiste avec effroi au rétrécissement inexorable des appartements. On en veut presque à Vian d\'être aussi lucide et de ne pas s\'être contenté d\'une expérience ludique sur fond de roman d\'amour. --Sana Tang-Léopold Wauters<\/span>\n  <a </div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16307683419633474415">Romain Gary was a Jewish-French novelist, film director, World War II aviator and diplomat. He also wrote under the pen name <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6576543.Emile_Ajar' rel='nofollow noopener'>Émile Ajar</a>.<br/><br/>Born Roman Kacew (Yiddish: קצב, Russian: Кацев), Romain Gary grew up in Vilnius to a family of Lithuanian Jews. He changed his name to Romain Gary when he escaped occupied France to fight with Great Britain against Germany in WWII. His father, Arieh-Leib Kacew, ab</span>
  <span id="freeText16307683419633474415" style="display:none">Romain Gary was a Jewish-French novelist, film director, World War II aviator and diplomat. He also wrote under the pen name <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6576543.Emile_Ajar" rel="nofollow noopener">Émile Ajar</a>.<br /><br />Born Roman Kacew (Yiddish: קצב, Russian: Кацев), Romain Gary grew up in Vilnius to a family of Lithuanian Jews. He changed his name to Romain Gary when he escaped occupied France to fight with Great Britain against Germany in WWII. His father, Arieh-Leib Kacew, abandoned his family in 1925 and remarried. From this time Gary was raised by his mother, Nina Owczinski. When he was fourteen, he and his mother moved to Nice, France. In his books and interviews, he presented many different versions of his father's origin, parents, occupation and childhood.<br /><br />He later studied law, first in Aix-en-Provence and then in Paris. He learned to pilot an aircraft in the French Air Force in Salon-de-Provence and in Avord Air Base, near Bourges. Following the Nazi occupation of France in World War II, he fled to England and under Charles de Gaulle served with the Free French Forces in Europe and North Africa. As a pilot, he took part in over 25 successful offensives logging over 65 hours of air time.<br /><br />He was greatly decorated for his bravery in the war, receiving many medals and honours.<br /><br />After the war, he worked in the French diplomatic service and in 1945 published his first novel. He would become one of France's most popular and prolific writers, authoring more than thirty novels, essays and memoirs, some of which he wrote under the pseudonym of Émile Ajar. He also wrote one novel under the pseudonym of Fosco Sinibaldi and another as Shatan Bogat.<br /><br />In 1952, he became secretary of the French Delegation to the United Nations in New York, and later in London (in 1955).<br /><br />In 1956, he became Consul General of France in Los Angeles.<br /><br />He is the only person to win the Prix Goncourt twice. This prize for French language literature is awarded only once to an author. Gary, who had already received the prize in 1956 for Les racines du ciel, published La vie devant soi under the pseudonym of Émile Ajar in 1975. The Académie Goncourt awarded the prize to the author of this book without knowing his real identity. A period of literary intrigue followed. Gary's little cousin Paul Pavlowitch posed as the author for a time. Gary later revealed the truth in his posthumous book Vie et mort d'Émile Ajar.<br /><br />Gary's first wife was the British writer, journalist, and Vogue editor Lesley Blanch (author of The Wilder Shores of Love). They married in 1944 and divorced in 1961. From 1962 to 1970, Gary was married to the American actress Jean Seberg, with whom he had a son, Alexandre Diego Gary.<br /><br />He also co-wrote the screenplay for the motion picture, The Longest Day and co-wrote and directed the 1971 film Kill!, starring his now ex-wife Seberg.<br /><br />Suffering from depression after Seberg's 1979 suicide, Gary died of a self-inflicted gunshot wound on December 2, 1980 in Paris, France though he left a note which said specifically that his death had no relation with Seberg's suicide.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16307683419633474415" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
